There are a number of different facets to data governance, but for the strict purposes of this course, we are going to focus on two main pillars: data protection and intellectual property. Some of what I will say right now will sound very familiar to those of you who already took the dedicated data protection course last semester. However, for the purposes of our current course, I am going to be a lot more condensed. We are not going to dive into all the granular legal details for exactly how data protection works in court.

    In terms of your first task—data protection—what you need to do as a system designer is govern your use of data to absolutely avoid violating data protection laws. Unless you have a formal legal background, my main recommendation is simply that you should be highly aware of what the general rules are, so that you are able to identify questions for a formal legal review. Unless you are quite confident, you do not actually want to try to answer the complex legal questions yourself because you may get the answer wrong. In the corporate legal context, getting these answers wrong results in severe legal sanctions and financial penalties. So, just bear that in mind. For the exam scenario I will give you in this course, I will not expect you to provide the definitive legal answers, but I absolutely want you to highlight the critical questions and red flags that you think may come up.

    Before we go further, it is crucial to note that data protection laws differ significantly from country to country and from jurisdiction to jurisdiction. There is no one single global data protection law that solves everything. Therefore, I will not be looking at any one particular country's specific statute. Instead, I will highlight some of the universal general data protection principles. These principles are mostly fleshed out and listed in the ASEAN Framework on Personal Data Protection. This framework serves as a kind of soft agreement among ASEAN Member States to say that they will endeavor to align their domestic data protection laws based on these shared general principles. You do not have to memorize the entire document; I will just go through the core principles right now, and you can take notes based on what we discuss.

    我们刚刚结束了评估公平性的一种可能工具，即反事实公平性评估。现在，我们要转而探讨数据治理。正如该原则的名称所示，数据治理意味着你要管理你的人工智能系统如何处理数据，特别是你的系统正在处理哪些数据。数据治理有许多不同的方面，但严格就本课程而言，我们将重点关注两大支柱：数据保护和知识产权。对于那些上个学期已经修过专门的数据保护课程的同学来说，我现在要说的一些内容听起来会非常熟悉。然而，为了我们当前课程的需要，我将会把内容浓缩得多。我们不会深入探讨数据保护在法庭上具体如何运作的所有繁琐的法律细节。

    关于你们的首要任务——数据保护——作为系统设计者，你需要做的是管理你对数据的使用，以绝对避免违反数据保护法。除非你有正规的法律背景，否则我的主要建议是：你应该高度了解一般规则是什么，这样你才能识别出需要进行正式法律审查的问题。除非你非常有把握，否则你其实并不想自己去尝试回答那些复杂的法律问题，因为你可能会得出错误的答案。在企业法律环境中，弄错这些答案会导致严厉的法律制裁和财务处罚。所以，请牢记这一点。在本课程我将给你们的考试场景中，我不会期望你们提供最终的法律答案，但我绝对希望你们能指出你们认为可能会出现的关键问题和危险信号。

    在我们继续之前，必须要指出的是，数据保护法因国家和司法管辖区的不同而有显著差异。没有一部单一的全球数据保护法能解决所有问题。因此，我不会着眼于任何一个特定国家的具体法规。相反，我将强调一些普遍的、一般性的数据保护原则。这些原则主要在《东盟个人数据保护框架》中得到了充实和列举。该框架作为东盟成员国之间的一种软性协议，表明他们将努力根据这些共享的一般原则来统一国内的数据保护法。你们不需要记住整份文件；我现在只会过一遍核心原则，你们可以根据我们讨论的内容做笔记。

  - [13:05:24 - 13:11:07] **Defining Personal Data, Anonymization, and Biometrics**

    Before diving directly into the principles, there is a fundamental preliminary question you must ask when looking at things from a data protection perspective: Does the data your AI system is using actually constitute "personal data"? If the data is indeed personal data or personal information, then that data will be caught by data protection law. If it is not personal data, then you simply do not have to worry about data protection law at all. Broadly speaking, personal data refers to any data that relates to an individual who can be identified from that data.

    Could you suggest maybe five examples of what you might consider to be personal data?

    *A student responds, suggesting classic identifiers like a full name, and personal identification numbers like a passport number or a Singaporean NRIC number.*

    Yes, those are the main, classic types of personal data. Your full name relates directly to you and allows you to be identified. An ID number, like a passport number or an IC number if you are Singaporean, are definitely personal data.

    *Another student suggests financial data, like a person's salary, and another suggests demographic traits like age or race.*

    Okay, financial data like your salary may or may not be personal data. Your salary relates to you because that is what you are earning, but whether it is legally personal data depends entirely on whether you are identifiable from it. If there is a dataset that includes your name right next to your salary, then it points directly to you and is personal data. But if it is just a standalone number representing a salary with no other context, you wouldn't be identifiable, so it would not be personal data. The same applies to race, age, and other characteristics. To the extent that these traits can be attached to something like a name or an ID number that singles you out, they become personal data. As long as your data falls within this definition, you must govern it carefully.

    This brings me to another critical concept: anonymization. Anonymization refers to the deliberate process of converting personal data into non-personal data. If you have a massive dataset and you want to use it to train your AI model, you naturally do not want to have to deal with the heavy burden of data protection law. What you might want to do is anonymize the dataset. This means you mathematically or systematically take away the names and any other means of identifying a particular individual. Once you successfully anonymize the dataset, it becomes "clean," and you can use it for whatever algorithmic training you want without having to care about data protection regulations. However, you must be extremely careful with this. Anonymization is not straightforward. It is not as easy as simply deleting the "Name" column in an Excel sheet. Even if you remove the explicit name, it may still be completely possible to identify the individual just by cross-referencing the other unique characteristics left in the row.

    *A student raises a question specifically about anonymization regarding carebots. The student notes that a carebot takes a picture to recognize a user, but its privacy policy claims it only stores "algorithmically processed features of your face" rather than the actual raw image file. The student asks if this counts as anonymization.*

    That is a great question dealing with biometrics. Usually, what biometric systems do is take a raw image of your face, a fingerprint, or even DNA, and then they algorithmically break it down into a specific set of mathematical data points. That unique set of data points is then stored in the system and used to detect if you are who you say you are. Does transforming a photo into data points count as anonymizing the data? I think the answer is firmly yes, it is still personal data. Even though it is heavily processed, that specific set of data points is uniquely designed to mathematically identify *you* and only you. Therefore, biometric data points are just a highly processed form of personal data, and they firmly fall under data protection laws.

    在直接深入探讨这些原则之前，从数据保护的角度来看待问题时，你必须问一个基本的首要问题：你的人工智能系统正在使用的数据是否真正构成了“个人数据”？如果这些数据确实是个人数据或个人信息，那么这些数据将受到数据保护法的约束。如果它不是个人数据，那么你根本就不需要担心数据保护法。广义上讲，个人数据指的是与任何能够从中被识别出身份的个人相关的数据。

    你们能举出五个你们认为是个人数据的例子吗？

    *一位学生回答，提出了经典的标识符，如全名，以及个人识别号码，如护照号码或新加坡身份证（NRIC）号码。*

    是的，这些是主要的、经典的个人数据类型。你的全名直接与你相关，并且能让人识别出你。身份证号码，比如护照号码，或者如果你是新加坡人的话就是身份证（IC）号码，绝对都是个人数据。

    *另一位学生提出了财务数据，比如个人的薪水，还有一位学生提出了人口统计学特征，比如年龄或种族。*

    好的，像你的薪水这样的财务数据可能是也可能不是个人数据。你的薪水与你有关，因为那是你赚的钱，但它在法律上是否属于个人数据，完全取决于你是否能从中被识别出来。如果有一个数据集包含了你的名字，紧挨着你的薪水，那么它就直接指向了你，这就是个人数据。但如果它只是一个代表薪水的独立数字，没有其他上下文，你将无法被识别，那么它就不会是个人数据。同样的道理也适用于种族、年龄和其他特征。只要这些特征可以附加到像名字或身份证号码这样能把你挑选出来的东西上，它们就变成了个人数据。只要你的数据属于这个定义范围，你就必须谨慎地管理它。

    这就引出了另一个关键概念：匿名化。匿名化指的是将个人数据转化为非个人数据的刻意过程。如果你有一个庞大的数据集，并且你想用它来训练你的人工智能模型，你自然不希望去处理数据保护法带来的沉重负担。你可能想做的就是对数据集进行匿名化。这意味着你要在数学上或系统上删除名字以及任何其他识别特定个人的手段。一旦你成功地对数据集进行了匿名化，它就变“干净”了，你就可以把它用于任何你想要的算法训练，而无需关心数据保护法规。然而，你必须对此极其小心。匿名化并非直截了当。它不像仅仅在Excel表格中删除“姓名”列那么简单。即使你删除了明确的名字，仅仅通过交叉引用该行中留下的其他独特特征，可能仍然完全可以识别出该个人。

    *一名学生专门提出了一个关于护理机器人匿名化的问题。该学生指出，护理机器人会拍照来识别用户，但其隐私政策声称它只存储“经过算法处理的面部特征”，而不是实际的原始图像文件。学生问这是否算作匿名化。*

    这是一个涉及生物识别技术的绝佳问题。通常，生物识别系统所做的是获取你面部、指纹甚至DNA的原始图像，然后通过算法将其分解成一组特定的数学数据点。那组独特的数据点随后被存储在系统中，用于检测你是否就是你所说的那个人。将照片转化为数据点算作对数据进行匿名化吗？我认为答案非常肯定，它仍然是个人数据。尽管经过了大量处理，但这组特定的数据点是专门设计用来在数学上识别出“你”且仅仅是你的。因此，生物识别数据点只是一种经过高度处理的个人数据形式，它们牢牢属于数据保护法的管辖范围。

  - [13:11:07 - 13:15:49] **Principle 1 & 2: Consent, Notification, Purpose, and Accuracy**

    Now that we know what personal data is, let us look at the principles. The first principle from the ASEAN framework is the principle of Consent, Notification, and Purpose. What this means is that an organization should not collect, use, or disclose personal data about an individual unless they have properly notified the individual and obtained their explicit consent. You need to tell the individual, "Here is exactly what I want to do with your personal data," and then the individual must tick a box or sign a form granting permission. For example, when you enrolled into NUS, the university gave you a massive form—which you may or may not actually remember reading—and you signed it indicating that you consent to NUS processing your personal data for specific administrative purposes. The personal data notice explicitly lists that data will be collected, used, and disclosed for purposes A, B, C, D, all the way down. Generally, you must give notice and acquire consent unless a specific domestic law or regulation authorizes collection without it.

    I want to ask the class: can you think of why this strict requirement of notice and consent creates massive, special problems specifically in the context of developing AI systems?

    *A student responds, pointing out that AI depends on astronomically large amounts of data for training. The student argues that unless the training is done entirely on public datasets, going to every single individual—say, millions of people—to get individual consent forms signed would be physically impossible and a massive bottleneck.*

    Exactly. This is one of the major hurdles that arises when AI developers want to use vast repositories of personal data to train their massive AI models. The fundamental problem is that you cannot realistically locate and get bespoke consent from every single individual in a dataset containing millions of rows. Therefore, developers either have to strictly anonymize the data, rely on purely publicly available datasets, or find a specific legal exception to operate under.

    Related to this is the concept of purpose. An organization may only collect, use, or disclose personal data for purposes that a reasonable person would consider appropriate under the circumstances. If you secretly use collected personal data to do something blatantly illegal or to intentionally harm the individual in some way, that is clearly inappropriate and a severe violation. However, for standard AI system training, you usually shouldn't run into a fundamental problem with the "appropriate purpose" test, provided you are transparent.

    The second principle is the Accuracy of Personal Data. This principle dictates that the personal data you hold should be accurate and complete to the extent necessary for the specific purpose for which it is being used. This means that if you are going to base a significant automated decision on personal data, that data cannot be riddled with errors. For example, if NUS is using an automated system to take in your applications for university admission, NUS has a legal obligation to ensure that the academic data it is collecting about you is highly accurate and complete, so that the system can reliably come to the correct decision as to whether to admit you or not.

    现在我们知道什么是个人数据了，让我们来看看这些原则。东盟框架中的第一个原则是同意、通知和目的原则。这意味着，除非组织已适当地通知了个人并获得了他们明确的同意，否则组织不应收集、使用或披露有关个人的个人数据。你需要告诉个人：“这正是我打算用你的个人数据做的事情”，然后个人必须勾选一个框或签署一份表格来授予许可。例如，当你注册进入新加坡国立大学（NUS）时，大学给了你一份长长的表格——你可能记得也可能根本不记得自己读过——你签署了它，表明你同意NUS出于特定的行政目的处理你的个人数据。个人数据通知中明确列出了数据将被收集、使用和披露用于目的A、B、C、D等等。一般来说，你必须发出通知并获得同意，除非特定的国内法律或法规授权在没有通知和同意的情况下进行收集。

    我想问问大家：你们能想到为什么通知和同意这一严格要求，会特别在开发人工智能系统的背景下造成巨大的、特殊的问题吗？

    *一名学生回答，指出人工智能的训练依赖于天文数字般庞大的数据量。该学生认为，除非训练完全在公共数据集上进行，否则去找每一个个人——比方说数百万的人——去签署个人同意书，在物理上是不可能的，而且会是一个巨大的瓶颈。*

    完全正确。当人工智能开发者想要利用海量的个人数据存储库来训练他们庞大的人工智能模型时，这就是出现的主要障碍之一。根本问题在于，在包含数百万行的数据集中，你无法现实地定位并获得每一个个人的定制同意。因此，开发者要么必须对数据进行严格的匿名化，要么依赖纯粹公开可用的数据集，要么找到特定的法律例外情况来开展运作。

    与此相关的是目的的概念。组织仅可出于一个理性的人在当前情况下认为适当的目的，来收集、使用或披露个人数据。如果你秘密地使用收集到的个人数据去做公然违法的事情，或者以某种方式故意伤害个人，这显然是不适当的，并且是严重的违规行为。然而，对于标准的人工智能系统训练来说，只要你是透明的，通常你在“适当目的”的检验上不应该遇到根本性的问题。

    第二个原则是个人数据的准确性。该原则规定，你所掌握的个人数据应当准确且完整，其程度必须满足其被使用的特定目的之需。这意味着，如果你打算基于个人数据做出重大的自动化决策，那么这些数据就不能错误百出。例如，如果NUS使用自动化系统来接收你们的大学入学申请，NUS就有法律义务确保它所收集的关于你的学术数据是高度准确和完整的，这样系统才能可靠地得出是否录取你的正确决定。

  - [13:15:49 - 13:21:00] **Principles 3 to 7: Security, Access, Transfer, Retention, and Accountability**

    The third principle focuses on Security Safeguards. This principle states that personal data should be appropriately protected against loss, unauthorized access, collection, use, disclosure, copying, modification, or destruction. This overlaps quite substantially with our previous technical lessons on AI security and robustness. You must build technical guardrails to prevent hackers from leaking the data, maliciously extracting the data, or corrupting it.

    Next is the Access and Correction principle. This essentially guarantees that upon request by an individual, an organization must provide the individual access to their personal data that is in the organization's possession within a reasonable period of time. Furthermore, "correction" means that the individual has the legal right to ask the organization to correct any errors or omissions in their personal data. Both of these mechanisms are aimed at ensuring that individuals are empowered to fix any detrimental mistakes that may be lurking inside your algorithmic datasets.

    Moving on, we have the principle governing Transfers to Another Country or Territory. In the digital economy, data crosses borders constantly. This issue arises when you are transferring personal data from a server in Singapore, for instance, to a data center in a completely different country. The principle mandates that before you execute an overseas transfer, you must either explicitly obtain the consent of the individual for that specific cross-border transfer, or you must take reasonable, binding steps to ensure that the receiving organization—let's say, a vendor located in Malaysia—will legally protect the personal data consistently with these exact same principles. You must ensure the data receives the same rigorous level of protection abroad as it would in its country of origin.

    The sixth principle is Retention. The core idea here is incredibly simple: when you no longer actively need the personal data for legal or business purposes, you must securely dispose of it. An organization should cease to retain documents containing personal data, or permanently remove the specific means by which the data can be associated with particular individuals, as soon as it is reasonable to assume retention is no longer necessary. You do not hold onto personal data unnecessarily indefinitely, because hoarding data creates massive, lingering risks that the personal data might be exposed in a future catastrophic breach.

    The final principle is Accountability. Accountability essentially means that an organization must take visible ownership of complying with these measures. You need to provide clear, easily accessible information to the public about your data protection policies and practices. You must also actively make information available on exactly how individuals can contact the organization to raise data protection concerns. Often, organizations fulfill this specific principle by publishing a comprehensive Data Protection Policy explicitly on their main website.

    Are there any questions about these fundamental data protection principles? Seeing none, let me reiterate that for the context of this course and the final exam, you do not have to go deep into the weeds of statutory legal details. What I mostly want you to do is that when you look at a hypothetical exam scenario, you should be able to actively highlight and spot the potential data protection issues hiding in the workflow based on these principles. I will not penalize you if you don't come out with the perfect legal answer on how to resolve it, just spot the risk.

    第三个原则侧重于安全保障。该原则规定，个人数据应受到适当的保护，以防止丢失、未经授权的访问、收集、使用、披露、复制、修改或破坏。这与我们之前关于人工智能安全性和稳健性的技术课程有很大的重叠。你必须建立技术护栏，以防止黑客泄露数据、恶意提取数据或破坏数据。

    接下来是访问与更正原则。这基本上保证了，在个人提出请求时，组织必须在合理的时间内让该个人访问组织所掌握的其个人数据。此外，“更正”意味着个人有法定权利要求组织更正其个人数据中的任何错误或遗漏。这两种机制都旨在确保个人有权纠正可能潜伏在你算法数据集中的任何有害错误。

    继续往下看，我们有管理转移到另一个国家或地区的原则。在数字经济中，数据不断跨越国界。当你将个人数据从新加坡的服务器转移到一个完全不同国家的数据中心时，就会出现这个问题。该原则强制规定，在执行海外转移之前，你必须明确获得个人对该特定跨境转移的同意，或者你必须采取合理的、具有约束力的措施，以确保接收组织——比方说，位于马来西亚的供应商——将按照这些完全相同的原则在法律上保护个人数据。你必须确保数据在国外获得与在其原产国相同严格级别的保护。

    第六个原则是保留。这里的核心理念极其简单：当你在法律或业务上不再积极需要这些个人数据时，你必须安全地将其销毁。一旦有理由认为保留不再是必要的，组织应立即停止保留包含个人数据的文件，或者永久删除能够将数据与特定个人联系起来的具体手段。你不能无限期地、不必要地保留个人数据，因为囤积数据会产生巨大的、挥之不去的风险，个人数据可能会在未来灾难性的泄露事件中被曝光。

    最后一个原则是问责制。问责制本质上意味着组织必须对遵守这些措施承担显而易见的责任。你需要向公众提供关于你的数据保护政策和做法的清晰、易于访问的信息。你还必须积极公开有关个人究竟如何联系组织以提出数据保护疑虑的信息。通常，组织通过在其主网站上明确发布全面的数据保护政策来履行这一特定原则。

    关于这些基本的数据保护原则还有什么问题吗？既然没有，让我重申一下，在本课程和期末考试的背景下，你们不需要深入探究法定法律细节的细枝末节。我最希望你们做的是，当你们看到一个假设的考试场景时，你们应该能够根据这些原则，积极地强调并发现隐藏在工作流程中的潜在数据保护问题。如果你们没有想出解决问题的完美法律答案，我不会扣分，只要发现风险就行。

  - [13:21:00 - 13:21:34] **Group Activity: The Screeni Hiring System**

    Okay, let us try this out in practice. We are going to do a quick exercise. Please direct your attention to the new scenario on the slide. We are returning to Oldbridge Hospital. In this case, Oldbridge Hospital receives far too many applications for its job openings and therefore wants to drastically accelerate its hiring process by using AI.

    To achieve this, Oldbridge decides to sign a contract with a third-party vendor called Screeni, which is a company legally located in the United States. Screeni provides an AI-driven service where their system will completely ingest all the relevant documentation submitted directly by the applicant—things like their CV, personal statement, and cover letter. But importantly, Screeni's system will also automatically harvest massive amounts of data from the open internet regarding the applicant. This includes scraping their social media posts, their LinkedIn profiles, and whatever other public information there is. The system digests all of this in order to automatically produce an "applicant report." This applicant report contains a generated summary of the applicant as well as a definitive quantitative score that algorithmically ranks how good the applicant is for the job.

    Based solely on this simple scenario setup, what specific questions of data protection can you identify? I want you to take the next five minutes to brainstorm and spot what data protection issues and principle violations you might want to raise for legal review.

    好的，让我们在实践中尝试一下。我们将做一个快速的练习。请把注意力集中在幻灯片上的新场景上。我们再次回到Oldbridge医院。在这个案例中，Oldbridge医院收到了太多针对其职位空缺的申请，因此希望通过使用人工智能来大幅加速其招聘过程。

    为了实现这一目标，Oldbridge决定与一家名为Screeni的第三方供应商签订合同，这是一家在法律上位于美国的公司。Screeni提供一种人工智能驱动的服务，他们的系统将完全摄取申请人直接提交的所有相关文件——比如他们的简历、个人陈述和求职信。但重要的是，Screeni的系统还会自动从开放的互联网上收集大量关于申请人的数据。这包括抓取他们的社交媒体帖子、他们的领英（LinkedIn）个人资料以及其他任何公开信息。系统消化所有这些信息，以自动生成一份“申请人报告”。这份申请人报告包含一份生成的申请人摘要，以及一个算法排名的明确量化分数，说明该申请人有多适合这份工作。

    仅仅基于这个简单的场景设置，你能识别出哪些具体的数据保护问题？我想让你们花接下来的五分钟时间进行头脑风暴，找出你们可能想要提交给法律审查的数据保护问题和违反原则的情况。

  - [13:27:27 - 13:30:47] **Data Protection Questions for the Screeni Scenario**

    Let us review the potential data protection issues you spotted in the Screeni hiring scenario. First, a student asked whether the fact that Screeni is located in the US—a third party in a different jurisdiction—changes the legal obligations. We do not have to go into exact statutory detail, but that question heavily depends on which specific national law we are analyzing. In certain jurisdictions, the fact that you are outsourcing processing to a foreign third party triggers massive compliance requirements, particularly concerning the principle of cross-border transfers.

    Another student brought up the principle of Retention. This is a massive issue in this scenario. Nothing in the prompt specifies how long Screeni or Oldbridge Hospital intends to hang on to this vast trove of applicant data. Are they keeping it indefinitely after the hiring cycle closes? That would be a direct violation of the retention principle.

    Then there is the issue of Accuracy, which becomes a severe problem specifically because of the automated data harvesting from the open internet. When you scrape social media and LinkedIn, the AI might pull in highly inaccurate data, severely outdated data, or, worst of all, data about a completely different person who happens to share the same name as the applicant. If the AI system accidentally attributes the incorrect person's digital footprint to the applicant, it completely corrupts the accuracy of the hiring report.

    Furthermore, how does the Access and Correction principle function here? If an applicant wants to access the sprawling dossier of data held by Screeni on US servers and correct a false assumption the AI made based on a scraped social media post, how do they realistically do that? Ensuring the applicant has a mechanism to access and correct their personal data must be explicitly included and governed in the vendor contract between Oldbridge and Screeni.

    Finally, a student asked about Accountability and whether Oldbridge is ultimately responsible for what Screeni does with the data. That is an excellent question. Again, it depends on the country, but in many strict jurisdictions, like Singapore for example, you are legally assumed to be fully responsible for whatever your outsourced third-party vendor is doing with the personal data you provided them. You cannot just wash your hands of the liability.

    So, those are the exact types of critical questions I want you to spot. You do not need to perfectly apply the legal provisions to solve them in the exam; you just need to accurately highlight that these data protection risks exist in the workflow.

    让我们回顾一下你在Screeni招聘场景中发现的潜在数据保护问题。首先，有位同学问，Screeni位于美国（即处于不同司法管辖区的第三方）这一事实是否会改变法律义务。我们不需要深入探讨确切的法定细节，但这个问题在很大程度上取决于我们正在分析的是哪个具体的国家法律。在某些司法管辖区，你将处理工作外包给外国第三方这一事实会引发大规模的合规要求，特别是涉及跨境转移原则的要求。

    另一位同学提出了保留原则。在这个场景中这是一个巨大的问题。提示中没有任何内容说明Screeni或Oldbridge医院打算将这批庞大的申请人数据保留多久。他们会在招聘周期结束后无限期地保留它吗？那将直接违反保留原则。

    然后是准确性问题，由于从开放的互联网上自动收集数据，这变成了一个严重的问题。当你抓取社交媒体和领英时，人工智能可能会引入极不准确的数据、严重过时的数据，或者最糟糕的是，引入与申请人碰巧同名的另一个完全不同的人的数据。如果人工智能系统意外地将错误的人的数字足迹归因于申请人，它将彻底破坏招聘报告的准确性。

    此外，访问和更正原则在这里是如何运作的？如果申请人想要访问Screeni在美国服务器上保存的关于他的庞大数据档案，并纠正人工智能基于抓取的社交媒体帖子做出的错误假设，他们在现实中该怎么做呢？确保申请人有机制访问和更正其个人数据，这必须明确包含在Oldbridge和Screeni之间的供应商合同中并受到管辖。

    最后，有位同学问到了问责制，以及Oldbridge最终是否要对Screeni处理数据的方式负责。这是一个很好的问题。同样，这取决于国家，但在许多严格的司法管辖区，比如新加坡，法律上假定你要为你外包的第三方供应商如何处理你提供给他们的个人数据承担全部责任。你不能就这么洗手不干，推卸责任。

    所以，这些正是我希望你们能发现的关键问题类型。你们不需要在考试中完美地运用法律条款来解决它们；你们只需要准确地指出工作流程中存在这些数据保护风险即可。

  - [13:30:47 - 13:35:57] **Intellectual Property and Copyright Law**

    That covers data protection. The second major pillar that pertains to data governance is Intellectual Property (IP). When you are mass-ingesting data to train your AI models, you must be highly vigilant to avoid violating intellectual property laws. Again, I do not expect you to come up with definitive legal IP answers in the exam; I just expect you to flag potential issues.

    Let me start by giving a brief explanation of what you should be looking for. Most of us have a general sense of what IP laws are. They protect distinct species of intellectual property. The "Big Three" IPs are trademark law, patent law, and copyright law. Trademark law protects brand identifiers like logos—for example, the famous McDonald's golden arches. Patent law protects functional inventions—for example, the underlying chemical formulas of the COVID-19 vaccines. However, as far as AI governance and machine learning are concerned, the main legal framework you are concerned with is copyright law. Copyright law protects creative, artistic, and literary works—for example, the novel *Pride and Prejudice*.

    You can see in the daily news reports that copyright violation is exactly what the massive AI companies are currently getting sued for. For example, the publishers of dictionaries are actively suing OpenAI for massive copyright infringement because OpenAI allegedly harvested and ingested the copyrighted text from those dictionaries to train ChatGPT. Disney and Universal are suing Midjourney over the unauthorized use of their copyrighted character images to train their image-generation models. And the music giant BMG is suing Anthropic for illegally using copyrighted song lyrics to train their AI models. All of these high-profile lawsuits come strictly under the purview of copyright law.

    To give you a concrete example of how copyright law mechanically works, let me briefly point you to the Singapore Copyright Act. You do not need to memorize the Act for the exam; copyright law is quite harmonized internationally, so most national copyright laws look fundamentally the same. The very first question you must ask when evaluating copyright risk is: does the thing your AI model is using legally classify as a "work"? Just like data protection law only covers "personal data," copyright law only covers "works."

    What actually counts as a work? The Act highlights a few classic categories: literary, dramatic, musical, or artistic works. Literary works include poems, novels, short stories, and articles—these are the primary things that Large Language Models have ingested in massive quantities. Artistic works include photographs, paintings, and drawings. In addition to these, the law also protects sound recordings, films, and broadcasts. As long as the material your AI model is being trained on falls within one of these protected categories, copyright law is potentially engaged.

    这就是数据保护的内容。与数据治理相关的第二个主要支柱是知识产权（IP）。当你在大规模摄取数据来训练你的人工智能模型时，你必须高度警惕以避免违反知识产权法。同样，我不期望你们在考试中想出明确的法定IP答案；我只期望你们能标记出潜在的问题。

    让我先简要解释一下你们应该寻找什么。我们大多数人对知识产权法是什么都有一个大致的概念。它们保护不同种类的知识产权。“三大”知识产权是商标法、专利法和版权法。商标法保护品牌标识符，如徽标——例如，著名的麦当劳金色拱门。专利法保护功能性发明——例如，COVID-19疫苗的底层化学配方。然而，就人工智能治理和机器学习而言，你所关心的主要法律框架是版权法。版权法保护具有创造性、艺术性和文学性的作品——例如小说《傲慢与偏见》。

    你可以从日常的新闻报道中看到，侵犯版权正是那些大型人工智能公司目前被起诉的原因。例如，词典出版商正积极起诉OpenAI大规模侵犯版权，因为据称OpenAI收集并摄取了这些词典中受版权保护的文本来训练ChatGPT。迪士尼和环球影业起诉Midjourney，指控其未经授权使用受版权保护的角色图像来训练其图像生成模型。音乐巨头BMG起诉Anthropic，指控其非法使用受版权保护的歌词来训练其人工智能模型。所有这些备受瞩目的诉讼都严格属于版权法的管辖范围。

    为了给你一个版权法在机制上如何运作的具体例子，让我简要地向你介绍一下《新加坡版权法》。你不需要为了考试去记住这部法令；版权法在国际上是相当统一的，所以大多数国家的版权法在根本上看起来是一样的。在评估版权风险时，你必须问的第一个问题是：你的人工智能模型正在使用的东西在法律上是否被归类为“作品”？就像数据保护法只涵盖“个人数据”一样，版权法只涵盖“作品”。

    究竟什么算作作品？该法令强调了几个经典的类别：文学、戏剧、音乐或艺术作品。文学作品包括诗歌、小说、短篇故事和文章——这些是大型语言模型（LLM）大量摄取的主要内容。艺术作品包括照片、绘画和图纸。除此之外，法律还保护录音、电影和广播。只要你的人工智能模型所训练的材料属于这些受保护的类别之一，就可能涉及版权法。

  - [13:35:57 - 13:45:31] **Infringement, Copying, and Fair Use**

    Once you establish that a "work" is involved, the next question is: are you committing a legal breach or infringement? An infringement arises when you perform an action that falls exclusively within the legal rights of the author or copyright owner without their explicit permission. For literary and artistic works, only the owner has the exclusive right to copy the work, publish the work, perform it in public, communicate it, or make an adaptation of it. As far as AI models and AI developers are concerned, the primary way you infringe copyright is by simply copying the work.

    But what exactly does it mean to "copy" in the digital age? Very briefly, to copy something is to make *any* reproduction of the work in *any* material form. For an AI developer, if you systematically scrape, download, and store the copyrighted work on your local computer or on a cloud server to build a training dataset, you have legally made a copy. Converting a physical work into a digital format is making a copy. Even if it is not a perfect 1-to-1 visual reproduction—for example, if you take a three-dimensional artistic sculpture and create a two-dimensional digital render of it—that is still legally classified as copying.

    Put yourself in the shoes of an AI developer. What are the typical circumstances where you might illegally copy a work? As a student noted, massive datasets like the 'Books3' corpus used by tech giants literally consist of millions of pirated digital copies of books dumped into training data. Making an unauthorized digital library to train an AI model is the most straightforward example of copyright infringement. Another massive issue arises with generative AI models. If a generative AI model outputs a piece of content that is substantially similar to an original copyrighted work it was trained on, that output itself can potentially be an infringement. There is an ongoing complex legal debate about who exactly owns the copyright if a user prompts an AI to write a novel—is it the user, the AI company, or nobody? But for this course, we are strictly concerned with making sure you, as the system designer, do not commit the initial infringement during the data ingestion and training phase.

    I will briefly mention one critical defense to copyright infringement: the notion of "Fair Use." Fair use is one of the most difficult and highly litigated aspects of copyright law. It is an affirmative defense. When a company like OpenAI is sued for copying millions of books, they will argue in court that their massive ingestion of data specifically for the purpose of machine learning constitutes a "fair use" of those works, and therefore is not a legal infringement.

    When evaluating a fair use defense, courts generally consider four complex factors. First, the purpose and character of the use—are you highly transforming the original work into something entirely new, or are you just making a bland, direct copy? Second, the nature of the copyrighted work—is it a highly creative artistic novel, or is it mostly factual data? Third, the amount and substantiality of the portion used—did you copy the entire 500-page book, or just a single short quote? And finally, the most important factor: what is the economic effect of your use on the potential market for the original work? If your AI generates a product that serves as a direct economic substitute for the original author's work, destroying their ability to sell it, the court is highly unlikely to grant you a fair use defense. You do not need to memorize these four factors, but understand that just because an AI company claims "fair use" does not mean they are automatically legally safe.

    一旦你确定涉及了“作品”，下一个问题是：你是否构成了法律上的违约或侵权？当你未经作者或版权所有者明确许可，执行了完全属于他们法定权利范围内的行为时，就构成了侵权。对于文学和艺术作品，只有所有者拥有复制作品、出版作品、公开表演作品、传播作品或对其进行改编的专有权利。就人工智能模型和人工智能开发者而言，你侵犯版权的主要方式仅仅就是复制作品。

    但在数字时代，“复制”到底意味着什么？简而言之，复制某物就是以“任何”物质形式对作品进行“任何”重制。对于人工智能开发者来说，如果你系统地抓取、下载并将受版权保护的作品存储在本地计算机或云服务器上以构建训练数据集，你在法律上就已经进行了复制。将实体作品转换为数字格式也是在进行复制。即使它不是完美的1比1视觉重现——例如，如果你拿了一个三维的艺术雕塑并创建了它的二维数字渲染图——这在法律上仍然被归类为复制。

    设身处地为人工智能开发者想一想。在哪些典型情况下你可能会非法复制作品？正如一位学生指出的，科技巨头使用的像“Books3”语料库这样的大型数据集，实际上就是由数百万本盗版数字图书组成的，它们被倾倒进训练数据中。制作未经授权的数字图书馆来训练人工智能模型是侵犯版权最直接的例子。生成式人工智能模型也引发了另一个巨大的问题。如果生成式人工智能模型输出的内容与它所训练的原始受版权保护的作品实质上相似，那么该输出本身就可能构成侵权。目前存在一场持续且复杂的法律争论：如果用户提示人工智能写一本小说，到底谁拥有版权——是用户、人工智能公司，还是谁也不属于？但在本课程中，我们严格关注的是确保你作为系统设计者，在数据摄取和训练阶段不会犯下最初的侵权行为。

    我将简要提及一个关于版权侵权的关键抗辩理由：“合理使用（Fair Use）”的概念。合理使用是版权法中最困难、诉讼最多的方面之一。这是一种积极抗辩。当像OpenAI这样的公司因复制数百万本书而被起诉时，他们会在法庭上辩称，他们专门为了机器学习的目的而大规模摄取数据构成了对这些作品的“合理使用”，因此不构成法律侵权。

    在评估合理使用抗辩时，法院通常会考虑四个复杂的因素。第一，使用的目的和性质——你是将原作品高度转化为全新的东西，还是仅仅进行平淡、直接的复制？第二，受版权保护作品的性质——它是一部极具创造性的艺术小说，还是主要是事实数据？第三，所使用部分的数量和实质性——你是复制了整本500页的书，还是仅仅引用了简短的一段话？最后，也是最重要的一个因素：你的使用对原作品潜在市场的经济影响是什么？如果你的人工智能生成的产品成为了原作者作品的直接经济替代品，破坏了他们销售作品的能力，那么法院极不可能批准你的合理使用抗辩。你不需要记住这四个因素，但要明白，仅仅因为人工智能公司声称“合理使用”，并不意味着他们在法律上就自动安全了。

  - [13:45:31 - 13:49:53] **Confidential Information and Implied Obligations**

    There is one final category of intellectual property law that you absolutely must be familiar with when governing AI data: the law of Confidential Information. This is the third major category of data you must handle with extreme care. Essentially, confidential information is sensitive information that you legally should not use or disclose except for very specific, authorized purposes.

    There are two main legal situations where misusing confidential information becomes highly problematic for an AI developer. The first, and easiest to understand, is when you have an explicit contract with the data owner. If you sign a commercial contract and you promise not to use their provided information outside of the agreed scope, you must follow that contract. For example, if a client hires your tech firm as a service provider and sends you a massive database of proprietary customer data to analyze, the contract almost certainly says, "I will not use this data for any purpose other than providing my specific service to you." If that is the case, you absolutely cannot secretly siphon off that proprietary data to train your own company's internal AI models without explicitly asking for and receiving written permission. That is a clear breach of contract.

    The more complicated situation arises when there is absolutely no formal written contract between you and the person who owns the data. Do not make the mistake of assuming that just because there is no contract, you can use the data for whatever you want. The courts recognize implied obligations of confidentiality based strictly on the circumstances under which the data was handed over to you. For example, if you and I are negotiating to form a joint venture, and I share my proprietary business data with you during those private negotiations, but the venture ultimately falls apart, you cannot just turn around and legally use my data to train your AI model. The law implies that the data was shared under a strict umbrella of confidentiality. Another classic example is the employer-employee relationship. If I hire you, the law automatically assumes you are obliged to keep my internal company information strictly confidential, even if you never signed a formal Non-Disclosure Agreement. The law will penalize you for misusing that confidential information. You don't need to memorize the exact legal statutes, just be highly alert to the possibility that business information may carry implied confidentiality when you are looking at an exam scenario.

    当你管理人工智能数据时，你绝对必须熟悉最后一类知识产权法：机密信息法。这是你必须极其小心处理的第三大类数据。从本质上讲，机密信息是敏感信息，除非为了非常具体、授权的目的，否则你在法律上不应使用或披露。

    在两种主要的法律情形下，滥用机密信息会给人工智能开发者带来极大的问题。第一种，也是最容易理解的，是当你与数据所有者签订了明确的合同时。如果你签署了一份商业合同，并且你承诺不在商定的范围之外使用他们提供的信息，你就必须遵守该合同。例如，如果客户聘请你的科技公司作为服务提供商，并发送给你一个庞大的专有客户数据库进行分析，合同几乎肯定会说：“除了向你提供我的具体服务外，我不会将此数据用于任何其他目的。”如果是这样的话，你绝对不能在没有明确要求并获得书面许可的情况下，秘密窃取这些专有数据来训练你公司内部的人工智能模型。这是明显的违约行为。

    更复杂的情况出现在你和数据所有者之间绝对没有正式书面合同的时候。不要错误地认为仅仅因为没有合同，你就可以想怎么用数据就怎么用数据。法院严格根据数据移交给你时的情况来确认保密的默示义务。例如，如果你和我正在谈判成立一家合资企业，并且在这些私人谈判期间我与你分享了我的专有业务数据，但合资企业最终破裂了，你不能转身就在法律上允许的范围内使用我的数据来训练你的人工智能模型。法律暗示该数据是在严格保密的情况下共享的。另一个经典的例子是雇主-雇员关系。如果我雇佣了你，法律会自动假定你有义务对我的公司内部信息严格保密，即使你从未签署过正式的保密协议（NDA）。法律将对你滥用机密信息的行为进行处罚。你不需要记住确切的法律法规，只需要在看考试场景时，对商业信息可能带有默示保密性的可能性保持高度警惕即可。

  - [13:49:53 - 13:54:01] **Group Activity: Raddi Radiograph Analytics IP Issues**

    Let us finish up with our last question and scenario for today. This question focuses specifically on identifying IP issues. In this scenario, Oldbridge Hospital contracts with a company called Raddi, which is a specialized radiograph analytics firm. Raddi provides a software service that uses an AI system to automatically analyze radiographic medical images—like X-rays and CT scans—to detect critical signs of disease such as tumors or internal bleeds. Crucially, Raddi explicitly wants to take the raw medical images provided by Oldbridge Hospital and use them as training data to continuously improve its own underlying AI model.

    Can you identify the specific IP and data governance questions that arise from Raddi taking this action?

    *A student responds, pointing out that the original medical scans can be considered copyrightable work. The student questions who actually owns the copyright to an X-ray, suggesting Raddi cannot just make copies without consent. Another student suggests that derivative works, like the generated medical reports, might also have IP implications.*

    Excellent. The first major issue that arises here is potential mass copyright infringement. The radiographic medical images—the X-rays and CT scans—are legally considered "artistic works" under copyright law, even though they are scientific and not "artistic" in the traditional creative sense. The owner of the copyright for these medical images is obviously Oldbridge Hospital, not Raddi. Therefore, Raddi absolutely cannot legally make copies of these images to feed into their training pipeline without securing an explicit copyright license from the hospital. Doing so would be blatant infringement.

    The second major issue you must spot is confidentiality. These radiographic images are inherently highly confidential medical records. Raddi would almost certainly have signed a vendor contract with Oldbridge Hospital that contains a strict contractual obligation explicitly forbidding them from using the hospital's private radiographic images for secondary commercial purposes, like training their own proprietary AI models. Furthermore, because these images were taken in a highly sensitive medical context, a massive obligation of medical confidentiality is owed directly to the individual patients themselves. This creates a complex web of third-party confidentiality breaches if Raddi misuses the scans.

    *A student then asks a follow-up question: since the medical scans inherently belong to the patient, would using them also be considered a breach of data protection laws alongside the IP issues?*

    Yes, absolutely. That is a fantastic point to end on. This specific scenario gives rise to severe IP *and* data protection problems simultaneously. To the extent that these medical images are identifiable—for instance, if the patient's name, birthdate, or medical record number is embedded in the metadata or burned into the corner of the X-ray scan—then those images legally constitute the patient's personal data. If Raddi uses them without the patient's explicit consent, they are violating core data protection principles like Notification and Consent, alongside their IP violations. In practice, it would be incredibly legally difficult for Raddi to justify scraping these raw images for model training.

    Any final questions on what we discussed today? Seeing none, that is all I have for today. Next week we will cover the final three principles of AI governance. My actual syllabus ends after next week's lesson, so my plan for the final session of the semester is to reserve it entirely as an open consultation block, just like I did previously. I will send out a form later for you to fill out if you want to book a consultation slot or have specific questions you want me to review. Thank you.

    让我们以今天的最后一个问题和场景来结束这节课。这个问题专门侧重于识别知识产权（IP）问题。在这个场景中，Oldbridge医院与一家名为Raddi的公司签订了合同，这是一家专业的放射图像分析公司。Raddi提供一种软件服务，该服务使用人工智能系统自动分析放射医学图像——如X光片和CT扫描——以检测肿瘤或内出血等关键的疾病迹象。关键的是，Raddi明确希望获取Oldbridge医院提供的原始医学图像，并将它们用作训练数据，以不断改进其自身的底层人工智能模型。

    你能指出Raddi采取这一行动所引发的具体知识产权和数据治理问题吗？

    *一名学生回答，指出原始的医学扫描可以被视为受版权保护的作品。该学生对谁真正拥有X光片的版权提出了质疑，认为Raddi不能在没有同意的情况下随意复制。另一名学生提出，衍生作品，如生成的医疗报告，也可能涉及知识产权问题。*

    非常好。这里出现的第一个主要问题是潜在的大规模版权侵权。根据版权法，放射医学图像——X光片和CT扫描——在法律上被视为“艺术作品”，尽管它们是科学的，而不是传统创造意义上的“艺术”。这些医学图像的版权所有者显然是Oldbridge医院，而不是Raddi。因此，Raddi绝对不能在法律上为了输入到他们的训练管道中而复制这些图像，除非从医院获得明确的版权许可。这样做将是公然的侵权。

    你必须发现的第二个主要问题是保密性。这些放射图像本质上是高度机密的医疗记录。Raddi几乎肯定会与Oldbridge医院签署一份供应商合同，其中包含严格的合同义务，明确禁止他们将医院的私人放射图像用于二次商业目的，比如训练他们自己的专有人工智能模型。此外，由于这些图像是在高度敏感的医疗环境中拍摄的，因此对个别患者本身负有重大的医疗保密义务。如果Raddi滥用这些扫描结果，就会造成错综复杂的第三方保密违约问题。

    *然后一名学生提出了一个后续问题：既然医疗扫描结果本质上属于患者，那么除了知识产权问题之外，使用它们是否也会被视为违反数据保护法？*

    是的，绝对是这样。以此作为结束是一个非常棒的观点。这个特定场景同时引发了严重的知识产权“和”数据保护问题。只要这些医学图像是可以识别出身份的——例如，如果患者的姓名、出生日期或病历号嵌入在元数据中或刻在X光扫描的角落里——那么这些图像在法律上就构成了患者的个人数据。如果Raddi在未经患者明确同意的情况下使用它们，他们不仅侵犯了知识产权，还违反了通知和同意等核心数据保护原则。在实践中，Raddi在法律上很难证明抓取这些原始图像用于模型训练是正当的。

    关于我们今天讨论的内容还有最后的问题吗？既然没有，这就是我今天讲的全部内容。下周我们将涵盖人工智能治理的最后三个原则。我的实际教学大纲在下周的课程后就结束了，所以对本学期最后一节课的计划是，将其完全保留为一个公开咨询时间，就像我之前做的那样。稍后我会发一份表格供你们填写，如果你们想预约咨询时间或有具体问题想让我回顾的话。谢谢。
***

# Reading
## Reading Week 1
  - Introduction to AI
    - Adriana Placani, ‘Anthropomorphism in AI: hype and fallacy’ (2024) 4 AI and Ethics 691.
    - Alva Markelius and others, ‘The mechanisms of AI hype and its planetary and social costs’ (2024) 4 AI and Ethics 727.
    - Blake Richards and others, ‘The Illusion of AI’s Existential Risk’ (Noema, 2023).
  - Introduction to AI ethics
    - Sheila Bonde and Paul Firenze, ‘A Framework for Making Ethical Decisions’ (2013).
    - (OPTIONAL) Frank Aragbonfoh Abumere, ‘Utilitarianism’ in George Matthews (ed), Introduction to Philosophy: Ethics (2019).
    - (OPTIONAL) Joseph Kranak, ‘Kantian Deontology’ in George Matthews (ed), Introduction to Philosophy: Ethics (2019).
    - (OPTIONAL) Douglas Giles, ‘How Can I Be a Better Person? On Virtue Ethics’ in George Matthews (ed), Introduction to Philosophy: Ethics (2019).
  - Introduction to AI governance
    - Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development, ‘Recommendation of the Council on Artificial Intelligence’ (2025), section 1.
    - Association of Southeast Asian Nations, ‘ASEAN Guide on AI Governance and Ethics’ (2024) at pages 10-16.
    - Marco Pasqua, ‘Bridging Soft and Hard Law in AI Governance’ (2025).
## Reading Week 2
  - Ethics – autonomous weapons
    - Peter Asaro, ‘Autonomous Weapons and the Ethics of Artificial Intelligence’ in S Matthew Liao (ed), Ethics of Artificial Intelligence (Oxford University Press 2020).
    - Michael Haiden and Florian Richter, ‘Autonomous weapons: considering the rights and interests of soldiers’ (2025) 27 Ethics and Information Technology 52.
  - Ethics – autonomous vehicles
    - Sven Ove Hansson, Matts-Åke Belin and Björn Lundgren, ‘Self-Driving Vehicles – an Ethical Overview’ (2021) 34 Philosophy and Technology 1383.
    - Alexander Hevelke and Julian Nida-Rümelin, ‘Responsibility for Crashes of Autonomous Vehicles: An Ethical Analysis’ (2015) 21 Science and Engineering Ethics 619.
    - Antje von Ungern-Sternberg, ‘Autonomous driving: regulatory challenges raised by artificial decision-making and tragic choices’ in Woodrow Barfield and Ugo Pagallo (eds), Research Handbook on the Law of Artificial Intelligence (Elgar 2018).
## Reading Week 3
  - **Ethics – AI-facilitated anti-competitive business practices.** What does it mean for a business practice to be anti-competitive? What anti- competitive business practices does AI facilitate, and how does AI facilitate those practices? Why are such anti-competitive business practices unethical?
    - Maurice E Stucke and Ariel Ezrachi, ‘Antitrust, algorithmic pricing and tacit collusion’ in Woodrow Barfield and Ugo Pagallo (eds), Research Handbook on the Law of Artificial Intelligence (Elgar 2018).
    - Thomas K Cheng and Julian Nowag, ‘Algorithmic Predation and Exclusion’ (2023) 25 University of Pennsylvania Journal of Business Law 41.
  - **Ethics – AI-facilitated personalised pricing.** What is personalised pricing? How does AI facilitate personalised pricing? Why might personalised pricing be unethical (and is it always so)?
    - Akiva A Miller, ‘What Do We Worry About When We Worry About Price Discrimination? The Law and Ethics of Using Personal Information for Pricing’ (2014) 19 Journal of Technology Law and Policy 41.
    - Jeffery Moriarty, ‘Why online personalized pricing is unfair’ (2021) 23 Ethics and Information Technology 495.
## Reading Week 4
  - **Ethics – Deepfakes** What are the harms of deepfakes, and are the ethical issues with deepfakes principally about their potentially harmful consequences? Might the making of deepfakes be intrinsically wrongful?
    - Adrienne de Ruiter, ‘The Distinct Wrong of Deepfakes’ (2021) 34 Philosophy & Technology 1311.
    - Daniel Story and Ryan Jenkins, ‘Deepfake Pornography and the Ethics of Non‑Veridical Representations’ (2023) 36 Philosophy & Technology 55.
    - Bobby Chesney and Danielle Citron, ‘Deep Fakes: A Looming Challenge for Privacy, Democracy, and National Security’ (2019) California Law Review 1753.
  - **Ethics – Facial recognition** What might be the ethical problems with the use of facial recognition technology? Does it matter (i) who is using it, (ii) where it is used, (iii) and why it is used? What are the social implications if the use of facial recognition technology is widespread?
    - Philip Brey, ‘Ethical Aspects of Facial Recognition Systems in Public Places’ (2004) 2 Journal of Information, Communication and Ethics in Society 97.
    - Evan Selinger and Brenda Leong, ‘The Ethics of Facial Recognition Technology’ in Carissa Véliz (ed), The Oxford Handbook of Digital Ethics (Oxford University Press 2024).
## Reading Week 5
  - **Ethics – legal automation** What are the risks of using AI in predictive policing? Should AI replace judges in court? What are the potential ethical problems with relying on AI to provide legal advice?
    - Andrew Guthrie Ferguson, ‘Policing Predictive Policing’ (2017) 94 Washington University Law Review 1109.
    - Tania Sourdin, ‘Judge v Robot? Artificial Intelligence and Judicial Decision- Making’ (2018) 41 UNSW Law Journal 1114.
    - Xin Dai, ‘Who Wants a Robo-Lawyer Now?: On AI Chatbots in China’s Public Legal Services Sector’ (2024) 26 Yale Journal of Law & Technology 527.
  - **Ethics – medical automation** What risks arise when AI is used for the provision of medical services? Would it be ethical to substitute a clinician with an AI tool?
    - Jens Christian Bjerring and Jacob Busch, ‘Artificial Intelligence and Patient- Centered Decision-Making’ (2021) 34 Philosophy & Technology 349.
    - Amitabha Palmer and David Schwan, ‘Advanced Technologies in Medicine: Safeguarding Physician Skill, Knowledge, and Character in the AI-Enhanced Clinic in Matthew C Altman and David Schwan (eds), Ethics and Medical Technology: Essays on Artificial Intelligence, Enhancement, Privacy, and Justice (Springer 2025).
## Reading Week 6
  - **Ethics – human-AI relationships** What are the personal ethical implications of the formation of human-AI relationships? Are there moral problems with creating companion AI, or allowing AI to be used in such a way?
    - Emilia Kaczmarek, ‘Self-Deception in Human-AI Emotional Relations’ (2025) 42 Journal of Applied Philosophy 814.
    - Kim Malfacini, ‘The impacts of companion AI on human relationships: risks, benefits, and design considerations’ (2025) 40 AI & Society 5527.
  - **Ethics – broader social implications** What are some ethical concerns with the use of AI for “social” scoring? How might AI shape the future of work, and what ethical implications do these shapings have?
    - Danielle Keats Citron and Frank Pasquale, ‘The Scored Society: Due Process for Automated Predictions’ (2014) 89 Washington Law Review 1.
    - John Danaher, ‘Automation and the Future of Work’ in Carissa Véliz (ed), The Oxford Handbook of Digital Ethics (Oxford University Press 2024).
## Reading Week 7
  - For the next few weeks, we will focus on AI governance. In particular, we will employ the AI Verify framework. Kindly access the framework here: https://file.go.gov.sg/aivtf-pdf.pdf
  - In preparation for the next few lessons, please read the following parts of the framework:
    - Seminar 7: Transparency, Explainability
    - Seminar 8: Repeatability/Reproducibility; Safety
    - Seminar 9: Security; Robustness
    - Seminar 10: Fairness; Data Governance
    - Seminar 11: Accountability; Human Agency & Oversight; Inclusive Growth, Societal, & Environmental Well-being
